American">Multiracial</a></td>\n<td>&#8211;</td>\n<td>&#8211;</td>\n<td>5.5%</td>\n<td>7.3%\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Languages">Languages</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Native_languages" title="Alaska Native languages">Alaska Native languages</a></div>\n<p>According to the 2011 <a href="/wiki/American_Community_Survey" title="American Community Survey">American Community Survey</a>, 83.4% of people over the age of five speak only English at home. About 3.5% speak Spanish at home. About 2.2% speak another <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European language</a> at home and about 4.3% speak an <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Asia" title="Languages of Asia">Asian</a> language (including <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">Tagalog</a>) at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> About 5.3% speak other languages at home.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Native_Language_Center" title="Alaska Native Language Center">Alaska Native Language Center</a> at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Alaska_Fairbanks" title="University of Alaska Fairbanks">University of Alaska Fairbanks</a> claims that at least 20 <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Native_languages" title="Alaska Native languages">Alaskan native languages</a> exist and there are also some languages with different dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uaf.edu_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-uaf.edu-55">[55]</a></sup> Most of Alaska\'s native languages belong to either the <a href="/wiki/Eskimo%E2%80%93Aleut_languages" title="Eskimo&#8211;Aleut languages">Eskimo&#8211;Aleut</a> or <a href="/wiki/Na-Dene_languages" title="Na-Dene languages">Na-Dene</a> language families however some languages are thought to be <a href="/wiki/Language_isolate" title="Language isolate">isolates</a> (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Haida_language" title="Haida language">Haida</a>) or have not yet been classified (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Tsimshianic_languages" title="Tsimshianic languages">Tsimshianic</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-uaf.edu_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-uaf.edu-55">[55]</a></sup> As of  2014<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alaska&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> nearly all of Alaska\'s native languages were classified as either threatened, shifting, moribund, nearly extinct, or dormant languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup>\n</p><p>A total of 5.2% of Alaskans speak one of the state\'s 20 <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_languages_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous languages of the Americas">indigenous languages</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> known locally as "native languages".\n</p><p>In October 2014, the governor of Alaska signed a bill declaring the state\'s 20 indigenous languages as official languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This bill gave the languages symbolic recognition as official languages, though they have not been adopted for official use within the government. The 20 languages that were included in the bill are:\n</p>\n<ol><li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Inupiat_language" title="Inupiat language">Inupiaq</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Central_Siberian_Yupik_language" title="Central Siberian Yupik language">Siberian Yupik</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Central_Alaskan_Yup%27ik_language" title="Central Alaskan Yup\'ik language">Central Alaskan Yup\'ik</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Alutiiq_language" title="Alutiiq language">Alutiiq</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Aleut_language" title="Aleut language">Unangax</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Dena%27ina_language" title="Dena\'ina language">Dena\'ina</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Deg_Xinag_language" title="Deg Xinag language">Deg Xinag</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Holikachuk_language" title="Holikachuk language">Holikachuk</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Koyukon_language" title="Koyukon language">Koyukon</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Upper_Kuskokwim_language" title="Upper Kuskokwim language">Upper Kuskokwim</a></li>\n<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gwich%27in_language" title="Gwich\'in language">Gwich\'in</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Lower_Tanana_language" title="Lower Tanana language">Tanana</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Upper_Tanana_language" title="Upper Tanana language">Upper Tanana</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tanacross_language" title="Tanacross language">Tanacross</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/H%C3%A4n_language" title="H&#228;n language">H&#228;n</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Ahtna_language" title="Ahtna language">Ahtna</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Eyak_language" title="Eyak language">Eyak</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Tlingit_language" title="Tlingit language">Tlingit</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Haida_language" title="Haida language">Haida</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/Coast_Tsimshian_dialect" title="Coast Tsimshian dialect">Tsimshian</a></li></ol>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="750" data-file-width="519" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg/170px-Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg/255px-Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/32/Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg/340px-Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sitka_-_St._Michael%27s_Russian_Orthodox_Cathedral.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/St._Michael%27s_Cathedral_(Sitka,_Alaska)" title="St. Michael\'s Cathedral (Sitka, Alaska)">St. Michael\'s Russian Orthodox Cathedral</a> in downtown <a href="/wiki/Sitka,_Alaska" title="Sitka, Alaska">Sitka</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3264" data-file-width="4928" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG/220px-Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG/330px-Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG/440px-Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Eagle_Baptist_Church.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold Rush-era <a href="/wiki/Baptists" title="Baptists">Baptist</a> church in <a href="/wiki/Eagle,_Alaska" title="Eagle, Alaska">Eagle</a></div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg/170px-Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg/255px-Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg/340px-Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Russian_Old_Believers_in_Nikolaevsk_AK_USA.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Russian <a href="/wiki/Old_Believers" title="Old Believers">Old Believers</a> Church in <a href="/wiki/Nikolaevsk,_Alaska" title="Nikolaevsk, Alaska">Nikolaevsk</a></div></div></div>\n<p>According to statistics collected by the Association of Religion Data Archives from 2010, about 34% of Alaska residents were members of religious congregations. 100,960 people identified as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evangelical_Protestant" title="Evangelical Protestant">Evangelical Protestants</a>, 50,866 as Roman Catholic, and 32,550 as mainline Protestants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-www.thearda.com_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-www.thearda.com-60">[60]</a></sup> Roughly 4% are Mormon, 0.5% are Jewish, 1% are Muslim, 0.5% are Buddhist, 0.2% are Bah&#225;\'&#237;, and 0.5% are Hindu.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup>  The largest religious denominations in Alaska as of  2010<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Alaska&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup> were the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> with 50,866 adherents, non-denominational Evangelical Protestants with 38,070 adherents, <a href="/wiki/The_Church_of_Jesus_Christ_of_Latter-day_Saints" title="The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints">The Church of Jesus Christ of Latter-day Saints</a> with 32,170 adherents, and the <a href="/wiki/Southern_Baptist_Convention" title="Southern Baptist Convention">Southern Baptist Convention</a> with 19,891 adherents.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup>  Alaska has been identified, along with Pacific Northwest states Washington and Oregon, as being <a href="/wiki/Unchurched_Belt" title="Unchurched Belt">the least religious states of the USA</a>, in terms of church membership,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1795, the First <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> was established in <a href="/wiki/Kodiak_Island_Borough,_Alaska" title="Kodiak Island Borough, Alaska">Kodiak</a>. Intermarriage with Alaskan Natives helped the Russian immigrants integrate into society. As a result, an increasing number of Russian Orthodox churches gradually became established within Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Alaska also has the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Quaker" title="Quaker">Quaker</a> population (by percentage) of any state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> In 2009 there were 6,000 Jews in Alaska (for whom observance of <a href="/wiki/Halakha" title="Halakha">halakha</a> <a href="/wiki/Jewish_law_in_the_polar_regions" title="Jewish law in the polar regions">may pose special problems</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Alaskan Hindus often share venues and celebrations with members of other Asian religious communities, including <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">Sikhs</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jains</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Estimates for the number of Muslims in Alaska range from 2,000 to 5,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup>  The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_Community_Center_of_Anchorage,_Alaska" title="Islamic Community Center of Anchorage, Alaska">Islamic Community Center of Anchorage</a> began efforts in the late 1990s to construct a mosque in Anchorage. They broke ground on a building in south Anchorage in 2010 and were nearing completion in late 2014. When completed, the mosque will be the first in the state and one of the northernmost mosques in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> There\'s also a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD" title="Bah&#225;\'&#237;">Bah&#225;\'&#237;</a> Center.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable sortable">\n<caption style="font-size:100%">Religious affiliation in Alaska (2014)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a title="Muslim">Muslim</a>\n</td>\n<td align="right"><b>0.5</b>\n</td>\n<td align="left"><span style="display:none;">0.5</span><div style="width:1px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:lightgreen">&#160;</div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:15px;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD" title="Bah&#225;\'&#237;">Bah&#225;\'&#237;</a>\n</td>\n<td align="right"><b>0.2</b>\n</td>\n<td align="left"><span style="display:none;">0.2</span><div style="width:0.4px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:lightgreen">&#160;</div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:15px;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a>\n</td>\n<td align="right"><b>1</b>\n</td>\n<td align="left"><span style="display:none;">1</span><div style="width:2px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:lightgreen">&#160;</div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:15px;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a>\n</td>\n<td align="right"><b>0.5</b>\n</td>\n<td align="left"><span style="display:none;">0.5</span><div style="width:1px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:lightgreen">&#160;</div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="text-align:left; text-indent:15px;">Other Non-Christian faiths\n</td>\n<td align="right"><b>4</b>\n</td>\n<td align="left"><span style="display:none;">4</span><div style="width:8px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:lightgreen">&#160;</div>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td>Don\'t know/refused answer\n</td>\n<td align="right"><b>1</b>\n</td>\n<td align="left"><span style="display:none;">1</span><div style="width:2px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:#A020F0">&#160;</div><b></b>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><b>Total</b></td>\n<td><b>100</b>\n</td>\n<td align="left"><span style="display:none;">100</span><div style="width:200px;height:2ex;background:#aaa;background:grey">&#160;</div><b></b>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Alaska" title="Economy of Alaska">Economy of Alaska</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_locations_by_per_capita_income" title="Alaska locations by per capita income">Alaska locations by per capita income</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="331" data-file-width="504" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg/220px-Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg/330px-Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg/440px-Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Prudhoe_Bay_aerial_FWS.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Aerial view of infrastructure at the <a href="/wiki/Prudhoe_Bay_Oil_Field" title="Prudhoe Bay Oil Field">Prudhoe Bay Oil Field</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The 2007 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gross_state_product" title="Gross state product">gross state product</a> was $44.9&#160;billion, 45th in the nation. Its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._states_by_GDP_per_capita_(nominal)" title="List of U.S. states by GDP per capita (nominal)">per capita personal income</a> for 2007 was $40,042, ranking 15th in the nation. According to a 2013 study by Phoenix Marketing International, Alaska had the fifth-largest number of millionaires per capita in the United States, with a ratio of 6.75 percent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> The oil and gas industry dominates the Alaskan economy, with more than 80% of the state\'s revenues derived from petroleum extraction. Alaska\'s main export product (excluding oil and natural gas) is seafood, primarily salmon, cod, Pollock and crab.\n</p><p>Agriculture represents a very small fraction of the Alaskan economy. Agricultural production is primarily for consumption within the state and includes nursery stock, dairy products, vegetables, and livestock. Manufacturing is limited, with most foodstuffs and general goods imported from elsewhere.\n</p><p>Employment is primarily in government and industries such as natural resource extraction, shipping, and transportation. Military bases are a significant component of the economy in the Fairbanks North Star, Anchorage and Kodiak Island boroughs, as well as Kodiak. Federal subsidies are also an important part of the economy, allowing the state to keep taxes low. Its industrial outputs are crude petroleum, natural gas, coal, gold, precious metals, zinc and other mining, seafood processing, timber and wood products. There is also a growing service and tourism sector. Tourists have contributed to the economy by supporting local lodging.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_in_Alaska" title="Natural gas in Alaska">Natural gas in Alaska</a> and <a href="/wiki/Energy_law#Alaska_law" title="Energy law">Energy law &#167;&#160;Alaska law</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg/170px-Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg/255px-Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg/340px-Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Pipeline_Closeup_Underneath.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Alaska_Pipeline" title="Trans-Alaska Pipeline">Trans-Alaska Pipeline</a> transports oil, Alaska\'s most financially important export, from the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_North_Slope" title="Alaska North Slope">North Slope</a> to <a href="/wiki/Valdez,_Alaska" title="Valdez, Alaska">Valdez</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Heat_pipe" title="Heat pipe">heat pipes</a> in the column mounts are pertinent, since they disperse heat upwards and prevent melting of <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Alaska has vast energy resources, although its oil reserves have been largely depleted. Major oil and gas reserves were found in the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_North_Slope" title="Alaska North Slope">Alaska North Slope</a> (ANS) and Cook Inlet basins, but according to the <a href="/wiki/Energy_Information_Administration" title="Energy Information Administration">Energy Information Administration</a>, by February 2014 Alaska had fallen to fourth place in the nation in crude oil production after Texas, North Dakota, and California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> Prudhoe Bay on Alaska\'s North Slope is still the second highest-yielding oil field in the United States, typically producing about 400,000 barrels per day (64,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d), although by early 2014 North Dakota\'s <a href="/wiki/Bakken_Formation" title="Bakken Formation">Bakken Formation</a> was producing over 900,000 barrels per day (140,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Prudhoe Bay was the largest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conventional_oil" title="Conventional oil">conventional oil</a> field ever discovered in North America, but was much smaller than Canada\'s enormous <a href="/wiki/Athabasca_oil_sands" title="Athabasca oil sands">Athabasca oil sands</a> field, which by 2014 was producing about 1,500,000 barrels per day (240,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>/d) of <a href="/wiki/Unconventional_oil" title="Unconventional oil">unconventional oil</a>, and had hundreds of years of producible reserves at that rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trans-Alaska_Pipeline" title="Trans-Alaska Pipeline">Trans-Alaska Pipeline</a> can transport and pump up to 2.1 million barrels (330,000&#160;m<sup>3</sup>) of crude oil per day, more than any other crude oil pipeline in the United States. Additionally, substantial coal deposits are found in Alaska\'s bituminous, sub-bituminous, and lignite coal basins. The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Geological_Survey" title="United States Geological Survey">United States Geological Survey</a> estimates that there are 85.4&#160;trillion cubic feet (2,420&#160;km<sup>3</sup>) of undiscovered, technically recoverable gas from natural gas hydrates on the Alaskan North Slope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> Alaska also offers some of the highest hydroelectric power potential in the country from its numerous rivers. Large swaths of the Alaskan coastline offer wind and geothermal energy potential as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r856303569">@media all and (max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .tmulti>.thumbinner{width:100%!important;max-width:none!important}.mw-parser-output .tmulti .tsingle{float:none!important;max-width:none!important;width:100%!important;text-align:center}}</style><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:438px;max-width:438px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:192px;max-width:192px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Crude_Oil_Reserves.PNG"><img alt="" data-file-height="771" data-file-width="633" decoding="async" height="231" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Reserves.PNG/190px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Reserves.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Reserves.PNG/285px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Reserves.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/62/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Reserves.PNG/380px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Reserves.PNG 2x" width="190"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Alaska <a href="/wiki/Proven_reserves" title="Proven reserves">proven oil reserves</a> peaked in 1978 and have declined more than 60% since then.</div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:242px;max-width:242px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG"><img alt="" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="668" decoding="async" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG/240px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG/360px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG/480px-Alaska_Crude_Oil_Production.PNG 2x" width="240"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left">Alaska oil production peaked in 1988 and has declined more than 65% since then.</div></div><div style="clear:left"></div></div></div>\n<p>Alaska\'s economy depends heavily on increasingly expensive diesel fuel for heating, transportation, electric power and light. Although wind and hydroelectric power are abundant and underdeveloped, proposals for statewide energy systems (e.g. with special <a href="/wiki/Single-wire_earth_return#Use_in_interties" title="Single-wire earth return">low-cost electric interties</a>) were judged uneconomical (at the time of the report, 2001) due to low (less than 50&#162;/gal) fuel prices, long distances and low population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> The cost of a gallon of gas in urban Alaska today is usually 30&#8211;60&#162; higher than the national average; prices in rural areas are generally significantly higher but vary widely depending on transportation costs, seasonal usage peaks, nearby petroleum development infrastructure and many other factors.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Permanent_Fund">Permanent Fund</span></h4>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Permanent_Fund" title="Alaska Permanent Fund">Alaska Permanent Fund</a> is a constitutionally authorized appropriation of oil revenues, established by voters in 1976 to manage a surplus in state petroleum revenues from oil, largely in anticipation of the then recently constructed <a href="/wiki/Trans-Alaska_Pipeline_System" title="Trans-Alaska Pipeline System">Trans-Alaska Pipeline System</a>. The fund was originally proposed by Governor <a href="/wiki/Keith_Harvey_Miller" title="Keith Harvey Miller">Keith Miller</a> on the eve of the 1969 Prudhoe Bay lease sale, out of fear that the legislature would spend the entire proceeds of the sale (which amounted to $900 million) at once. It was later championed by Governor <a href="/wiki/Jay_Hammond" title="Jay Hammond">Jay Hammond</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kenai,_Alaska" title="Kenai, Alaska">Kenai</a> <a href="/wiki/Alaska_House_of_Representatives" title="Alaska House of Representatives">state representative</a> Hugh Malone. It has served as an attractive political prospect ever since, diverting revenues which would normally be deposited into the general fund.\n</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_Constitution" title="Alaska Constitution">Alaska Constitution</a> was written so as to discourage dedicating state funds for a particular purpose. The Permanent Fund has become the rare exception to this, mostly due to the political climate of distrust existing during the time of its creation. From its initial principal of $734,000, the fund has grown to $50&#160;billion as a result of oil royalties and capital investment programs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Most if not all the principal is invested conservatively outside Alaska. This has led to frequent calls by Alaskan politicians for the Fund to make investments within Alaska, though such a stance has never gained momentum.\n</p><p>Starting in 1982, dividends from the fund\'s annual growth have been paid out each year to eligible Alaskans, ranging from an initial $1,000 in 1982 (equal to three years\' payout, as the distribution of payments was held up in a lawsuit over the distribution scheme) to $3,269 in 2008 (which included a one-time $1,200 "Resource Rebate"). Every year, the state legislature takes out 8% from the earnings, puts 3% back into the principal for inflation proofing, and the remaining 5% is distributed to all qualifying Alaskans. To qualify for the Permanent Fund Dividend, one must have lived in the state for a minimum of 12 months, maintain constant residency subject to allowable absences,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> and not be subject to court judgments or criminal convictions which fall under various disqualifying classifications or may subject the payment amount to civil garnishment.\n</p><p>The Permanent Fund is often considered to be one of the leading examples of a "<a href="/wiki/Basic_income" title="Basic income">Basic Income</a>" policy in the world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cost_of_living">Cost of living</span></h3>\n<p>The cost of goods in Alaska has long been higher than in the contiguous 48 states. Federal government employees, particularly <a href="/wiki/United_States_Postal_Service" title="United States Postal Service">United States Postal Service</a> (USPS) workers and active-duty military members, receive a Cost of Living Allowance usually set at 25% of base pay because, while the cost of living has gone down, it is still one of the highest in the country.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p><p>Rural Alaska suffers from extremely high prices for food and consumer goods compared to the rest of the country, due to the relatively limited transportation infrastructure.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Agriculture_and_fishing">Agriculture and fishing</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="1536" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG/170px-Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG/255px-Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG/340px-Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pacific_Halibut_Fileting.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Halibut" title="Halibut">Halibut</a> is important to the state\'s economy as both a commercial and sport-caught fish.</div></div></div>\n<p>Due to the northern climate and short growing season, relatively little farming occurs in Alaska. Most farms are in either the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Matanuska_Valley" title="Matanuska Valley">Matanuska Valley</a>, about 40 miles (64&#160;km) northeast of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anchorage" title="Anchorage">Anchorage</a>, or on the <a href="/wiki/Kenai_Peninsula" title="Kenai Peninsula">Kenai Peninsula</a>, about 60 miles (97&#160;km) southwest of Anchorage. The short 100-day growing season limits the crops that can be grown, but the long sunny summer days make for productive growing seasons. The primary crops are potatoes, carrots, lettuce, and cabbage.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Tanana_Valley" title="Tanana Valley">Tanana Valley</a> is another notable agricultural locus, especially the <a href="/wiki/Delta_Junction,_Alaska" title="Delta Junction, Alaska">Delta Junction</a> area, about 100 miles (160&#160;km) southeast of Fairbanks, with a sizable concentration of farms growing agronomic crops; these farms mostly lie north and east of <a href="/wiki/Fort_Greely" title="Fort Greely">Fort Greely</a>. This area was largely set aside and developed under a state program spearheaded by Hammond during his second term as governor. Delta-area crops consist predominantly of barley and hay. West of Fairbanks lies another concentration of small farms catering to restaurants, the hotel and tourist industry, and <a href="/wiki/Community-supported_agriculture" title="Community-supported agriculture">community-supported agriculture</a>.\n</p><p>Alaskan agriculture has experienced a surge in growth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Market_gardener" title="Market gardener">market gardeners</a>, small farms and <a href="/wiki/Farmers%27_market" title="Farmers\' market">farmers\' markets</a> in recent years, with the highest percentage increase (46%) in the nation in growth in farmers\' markets in 2011, compared to 17% nationwide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Peony" title="Peony">peony</a> industry has also taken off, as the growing season allows farmers to harvest during a gap in supply elsewhere in the world, thereby filling a niche in the flower market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r856303569" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:398px;max-width:398px"><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:192px;max-width:192px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heavy-lift_melon.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="143" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Heavy-lift_melon.jpg/190px-Heavy-lift_melon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Heavy-lift_melon.jpg/285px-Heavy-lift_melon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Heavy-lift_melon.jpg/380px-Heavy-lift_melon.jpg 2x" width="190"/></a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Monster_vegetable_display_at_the_Tanana_Valley_State_Fair_2010.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1566" data-file-width="2087" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Monster_vegetable_display_at_the_Tanana_Valley_State_Fair_2010.jpg/200px-Monster_vegetable_display_at_the_Tanana_Valley_State_Fair_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Monster_vegetable_display_at_the_Tanana_Valley_State_Fair_2010.jpg/300px-Monster_vegetable_display_at_the_Tanana_Valley_State_Fair_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d3/Monster_vegetable_display_at_the_Tanana_Valley_State_Fair_2010.jpg/400px-Monster_vegetable_display_at_the_Tanana_Valley_State_Fair_2010.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div></div><div style="clear:left"></div><div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left;text-align:left;background-color:transparent">Oversized vegetables on display at the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_State_Fair" title="Alaska State Fair">Alaska State Fair</a> (left) and the <a href="/wiki/Tanana_Valley_State_Fair" title="Tanana Valley State Fair">Tanana Valley State Fair</a> (right).</div></div></div>\n<p>Alaska, with no counties, lacks county fairs. However, a small assortment of state and local fairs (with the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_State_Fair" title="Alaska State Fair">Alaska State Fair</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palmer,_Alaska" title="Palmer, Alaska">Palmer</a> the largest), are held mostly in the late summer. The fairs are mostly located in communities with historic or current agricultural activity, and feature local farmers exhibiting produce in addition to more high-profile commercial activities such as carnival rides, concerts and food. "Alaska Grown" is used as an agricultural slogan.\n</p><p>Alaska has an abundance of seafood, with the primary fisheries in the Bering Sea and the North Pacific. Seafood is one of the few food items that is often cheaper within the state than outside it. Many Alaskans take advantage of salmon seasons to harvest portions of their household diet while fishing for subsistence, as well as sport. This includes fish taken by hook, net or wheel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Hunting for subsistence, primarily <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caribou" title="Caribou">caribou</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moose" title="Moose">moose</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dall_sheep" title="Dall sheep">Dall sheep</a> is still common in the state, particularly in remote <a href="/wiki/The_Bush_(Alaska)" title="The Bush (Alaska)">Bush</a> communities. An example of a traditional native food is <a href="/wiki/Akutaq" title="Akutaq">Akutaq</a>, the Eskimo ice cream, which can consist of reindeer fat, seal oil, dried fish meat and local berries.\n</p><p>Alaska\'s reindeer herding is concentrated on <a href="/wiki/Seward_Peninsula" title="Seward Peninsula">Seward Peninsula</a>, where wild caribou can be prevented from mingling and migrating with the domesticated reindeer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Most food in Alaska is transported into the state from <a href="/wiki/Outside_(Alaska)" title="Outside (Alaska)">"Outside"</a>, and shipping costs make food in the cities relatively expensive. In rural areas, subsistence hunting and gathering is an essential activity because imported food is prohibitively expensive. Although most small towns and villages in Alaska lie along the coastline, the cost of importing food to remote villages can be high, because of the terrain and difficult road conditions, which change dramatically, due to varying climate and precipitation changes. The cost of transport can reach as high as 50&#162; per pound ($1.10/kg) or more in some remote areas, during the most difficult times, if these locations can be reached at all during such inclement weather and terrain conditions. The cost of delivering a 1 US gallon (3.8&#160;L) of milk is about $3.50 in many villages where per capita income can be $20,000 or less. Fuel cost per gallon is routinely 20&#8211;30&#162; higher than the continental United States average, with only Hawaii having higher prices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Transportation">Transportation</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sterling_Highway.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Sterling_Highway.jpg/220px-Sterling_Highway.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Sterling_Highway.jpg/330px-Sterling_Highway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Sterling_Highway.jpg/440px-Sterling_Highway.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sterling_Highway.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Sterling_Highway" title="Sterling Highway">Sterling Highway</a>, near its intersection with the <a href="/wiki/Seward_Highway" title="Seward Highway">Seward Highway</a>.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Transportation_in_Alaska" title="Transportation in Alaska">Transportation in Alaska</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Alaska_Routes" title="List of Alaska Routes">List of Alaska Routes</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Susitnabridge.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1232" data-file-width="1632" decoding="async" height="166" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Susitnabridge.JPG/220px-Susitnabridge.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Susitnabridge.JPG/330px-Susitnabridge.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b5/Susitnabridge.JPG/440px-Susitnabridge.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Susitnabridge.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Susitna_River" title="Susitna River">Susitna River</a> bridge on the <a href="/wiki/Denali_Highway" title="Denali Highway">Denali Highway</a> is 1,036 feet (316&#160;m) long.</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Interstate_Alaska_map.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1286" data-file-width="2128" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Interstate_Alaska_map.png/220px-Interstate_Alaska_map.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Interstate_Alaska_map.png/330px-Interstate_Alaska_map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Interstate_Alaska_map.png/440px-Interstate_Alaska_map.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Interstate_Alaska_map.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highways_in_Alaska" title="Interstate Highways in Alaska">Alaska Interstate Highways</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Alaska has few road connections compared to the rest of the U.S. The state\'s road system covers a relatively small area of the state, linking the central population centers and the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Highway" title="Alaska Highway">Alaska Highway</a>, the principal route out of the state through Canada. The state capital, Juneau, is not accessible by road, only a car ferry, which has spurred several debates over the decades about moving the capital to a city on the road system, or building a road connection from <a href="/wiki/Haines,_Alaska" title="Haines, Alaska">Haines</a>. The western part of Alaska has no road system connecting the communities with the rest of Alaska.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:AlaskaSign.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/AlaskaSign.jpg/220px-AlaskaSign.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/AlaskaSign.jpg/330px-AlaskaSign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/a/a4/AlaskaSign.jpg/440px-AlaskaSign.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:AlaskaSign.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Alaska welcome sign on the <a href="/wiki/Klondike_Highway" title="Klondike Highway">Klondike Highway</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highways_in_Alaska" title="Interstate Highways in Alaska">Interstate Highways in Alaska</a> consists of a total of 1082 miles. One unique feature of the Alaska Highway system is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anton_Anderson_Memorial_Tunnel" title="Anton Anderson Memorial Tunnel">Anton Anderson Memorial Tunnel</a>, an active <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Railroad" title="Alaska Railroad">Alaska Railroad</a> tunnel recently upgraded to provide a paved roadway link with the isolated community of <a href="/wiki/Whittier,_Alaska" title="Whittier, Alaska">Whittier</a> on <a href="/wiki/Prince_William_Sound" title="Prince William Sound">Prince William Sound</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Seward_Highway" title="Seward Highway">Seward Highway</a> about 50 miles (80&#160;km) southeast of Anchorage at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Portage,_Alaska" title="Portage, Alaska">Portage</a>. At 2.5 miles (4.0&#160;km), the tunnel was the longest road tunnel in North America until 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> The tunnel is the longest combination <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_road-rail_tunnels" title="List of road-rail tunnels">road and rail tunnel</a> in North America.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Railroad,_Girdwood,_Alaska,_Estados_Unidos,_2017-08-31,_DD_40.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3366" data-file-width="5903" decoding="async" height="125" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Alaska_Railroad%2C_Girdwood%2C_Alaska%2C_Estados_Unidos%2C_2017-08-31%2C_DD_40.jpg/220px-Alaska_Railroad%2C_Girdwood%2C_Alaska%2C_Estados_Unidos%2C_2017-08-31%2C_DD_40.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Alaska_Railroad%2C_Girdwood%2C_Alaska%2C_Estados_Unidos%2C_2017-08-31%2C_DD_40.jpg/330px-Alaska_Railroad%2C_Girdwood%2C_Alaska%2C_Estados_Unidos%2C_2017-08-31%2C_DD_40.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Alaska_Railroad%2C_Girdwood%2C_Alaska%2C_Estados_Unidos%2C_2017-08-31%2C_DD_40.jpg/440px-Alaska_Railroad%2C_Girdwood%2C_Alaska%2C_Estados_Unidos%2C_2017-08-31%2C_DD_40.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Alaska_Railroad,_Girdwood,_Alaska,_Estados_Unidos,_2017-08-31,_DD_40.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Railroad" title="Alaska Railroad">Alaska Railroad</a> locomotive over a bridge in Girdwood approaching Anchorage (2007).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area,_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1562" data-file-width="2342" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area%2C_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg/220px-Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area%2C_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area%2C_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg/330px-Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area%2C_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area%2C_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg/440px-Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area%2C_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Skagway-Hoonah-Angoon_Census_Area,_In_to_the_Tunnel.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/White_Pass_and_Yukon_Route" title="White Pass and Yukon Route">White Pass and Yukon Route</a> traverses rugged terrain north of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Skagway" title="Skagway">Skagway</a> near the <a href="/wiki/Canada%E2%80%93United_States_border" title="Canada&#8211;United States border">Canada&#8211;US border</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Built around 1915, the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Railroad" title="Alaska Railroad">Alaska Railroad</a> (ARR) played a key role in the development of Alaska through the 20th century. It links north Pacific shipping through providing critical infrastructure with tracks that run from <a href="/wiki/Seward,_Alaska" title="Seward, Alaska">Seward</a> to <a href="/wiki/Interior_Alaska" title="Interior Alaska">Interior Alaska</a> by way of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Central_Alaska" title="South Central Alaska">South Central Alaska</a>, passing through Anchorage, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eklutna" title="Eklutna">Eklutna</a>, Wasilla, <a href="/wiki/Talkeetna,_Alaska" title="Talkeetna, Alaska">Talkeetna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Denali" title="Denali">Denali</a>, and Fairbanks, with spurs to <a href="/wiki/Whittier,_Alaska" title="Whittier, Alaska">Whittier</a>, <a href="/wiki/Palmer,_Alaska" title="Palmer, Alaska">Palmer</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Pole,_Alaska" title="North Pole, Alaska">North Pole</a>. The cities, towns, villages, and region served by ARR tracks are known statewide as "The Railbelt". In recent years, the ever-improving paved highway system began to eclipse the railroad\'s importance in Alaska\'s economy.\n</p><p>The railroad played a vital role in Alaska\'s development, moving freight into Alaska while transporting natural resources southward (i.e., coal from the Usibelli coal mine near <a href="/wiki/Healy,_Alaska" title="Healy, Alaska">Healy</a> to Seward and gravel from the Matanuska Valley to Anchorage). It is well known for its summertime tour passenger service.\n</p><p>The Alaska Railroad was one of the last railroads in North America to use <a href="/wiki/Caboose" title="Caboose">cabooses</a> in regular service and still uses them on some gravel trains. It continues to offer one of the last <a href="/wiki/Request_stop" title="Request stop">flag stop</a> routes in the country. A stretch of about 60 miles (100&#160;km) of track along an area north of Talkeetna remains inaccessible by road; the railroad provides the only transportation to rural homes and cabins in the area. Until construction of the Parks Highway in the 1970s, the railroad provided the only land access to most of the region along its entire route.\n</p><p>In northern Southeast Alaska, the <a href="/wiki/White_Pass_and_Yukon_Route" title="White Pass and Yukon Route">White Pass and Yukon Route</a> also partly runs through the state from <a href="/wiki/Skagway,_Alaska" title="Skagway, Alaska">Skagway</a> northwards into Canada (British Columbia and Yukon Territory), crossing the border at <a href="/wiki/White_Pass" title="White Pass">White Pass</a> Summit. This line is now mainly used by tourists, often arriving by cruise liner at Skagway. It was featured in the 1983 <a href="/wiki/BBC" title="BBC">BBC</a> television series <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Little_Railways" title="Great Little Railways">Great Little Railways</a>.</i>\n</p><p>The Alaska Rail network is not connected to Outside. In 2000, the U.S. Congress authorized $6&#160;million to study the feasibility of a rail link between Alaska, Canada, and the lower 48.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RailLink1_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-RailLink1-95">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RailLink2_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-RailLink2-96">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RailLink3_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-RailLink3-97">[97]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alaska_Rail_Marine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alaska Rail Marine (page does not exist)">Alaska Rail Marine</a> provides <a href="/wiki/Car_float" title="Car float">car float</a> service between <a href="/wiki/Whittier,_Alaska" title="Whittier, Alaska">Whittier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seattle" title="Seattle">Seattle</a>.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Marine_transport">Marine transport</span></h3>\n<p>Many cities, towns and villages in the state do not have road or highway access; the only modes of access involve travel by air, river, or the sea.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tustumena,_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Tustumena%2C_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg/220px-Tustumena%2C_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Tustumena%2C_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg/330px-Tustumena%2C_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Tustumena%2C_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg/440px-Tustumena%2C_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tustumena,_Alaska_Marine_Highway.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/MV_Tustumena" title="MV Tustumena">MV&#160;<i>Tustumena</i></a> (named after <a href="/wiki/Tustumena_Glacier" title="Tustumena Glacier">Tustumena Glacier</a>) is one of the state\'s many ferries, providing service between the <a href="/wiki/Kenai_Peninsula" title="Kenai Peninsula">Kenai Peninsula</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kodiak_Island" title="Kodiak Island">Kodiak Island</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aleutian_Chain" title="Aleutian Chain">Aleutian Chain</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>Alaska\'s well-developed state-owned ferry system (known as the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Marine_Highway" title="Alaska Marine Highway">Alaska Marine Highway</a>) serves the cities of <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Alaska" title="Southeast Alaska">southeast</a>, the Gulf Coast and the Alaska Peninsula. The ferries transport vehicles as well as passengers. The system also operates a ferry service from <a href="/wiki/Bellingham,_Washington" title="Bellingham, Washington">Bellingham, Washington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prince_Rupert,_British_Columbia" title="Prince Rupert, British Columbia">Prince Rupert, British Columbia</a>, in Canada through the <a href="/wiki/Inside_Passage" title="Inside Passage">Inside Passage</a> to <a href="/wiki/Skagway,_Alaska" title="Skagway, Alaska">Skagway</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Inter-Island_Ferry_Authority" title="Inter-Island Ferry Authority">Inter-Island Ferry Authority</a> also serves as an important marine link for many communities in the <a href="/wiki/Prince_of_Wales_Island_(Alaska)" title="Prince of Wales Island (Alaska)">Prince of Wales Island</a> region of Southeast and works in concert with the Alaska Marine Highway.\n</p><p>In recent years, cruise lines have created a summertime tourism market, mainly connecting the Pacific Northwest to Southeast Alaska and, to a lesser degree, towns along Alaska\'s gulf coast. The population of <a href="/wiki/Ketchikan,_Alaska" title="Ketchikan, Alaska">Ketchikan</a> may rise by over 10,000 people on many days during the summer, as up to four large cruise ships at a time can dock, debarking thousands of passengers.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_transport">Air transport</span></h3>\n<p>Cities not served by road, sea, or river can be reached only by air, foot, dogsled, or snowmachine, accounting for Alaska\'s extremely well developed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaskan_Bush" title="Alaskan Bush">bush</a> air services&#8212;an Alaskan novelty. Anchorage and, to a lesser extent Fairbanks, is served by <a href="/wiki/Ted_Stevens_Anchorage_International_Airport#Airlines_and_destinations" title="Ted Stevens Anchorage International Airport">many major airlines</a>. Because of limited highway access, air travel remains the most efficient form of transportation in and out of the state. Anchorage recently completed extensive remodeling and construction at <a href="/wiki/Ted_Stevens_Anchorage_International_Airport" title="Ted Stevens Anchorage International Airport">Ted Stevens Anchorage International Airport</a> to help accommodate the upsurge in tourism (in 2012&#8211;2013, Alaska received almost 2 million visitors).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Regular flights to most villages and towns within the state that are commercially viable are challenging to provide, so they are heavily subsidized by the federal government through the <a href="/wiki/Essential_Air_Service" title="Essential Air Service">Essential Air Service</a> program. <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Airlines" title="Alaska Airlines">Alaska Airlines</a> is the only major airline offering in-state travel with jet service (sometimes in combination cargo and passenger <a href="/wiki/Boeing_737" title="Boeing 737">Boeing 737</a>-400s) from Anchorage and <a href="/wiki/Fairbanks_International_Airport" title="Fairbanks International Airport">Fairbanks</a> to regional hubs like <a href="/wiki/Bethel,_Alaska" title="Bethel, Alaska">Bethel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nome,_Alaska" title="Nome, Alaska">Nome</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kotzebue,_Alaska" title="Kotzebue, Alaska">Kotzebue</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dillingham,_Alaska" title="Dillingham, Alaska">Dillingham</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kodiak,_Alaska" title="Kodiak, Alaska">Kodiak</a>, and other larger communities as well as to major Southeast and Alaska Peninsula communities.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_(6194226738).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1990" data-file-width="3319" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_%286194226738%29.jpg/220px-ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_%286194226738%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_%286194226738%29.jpg/330px-ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_%286194226738%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_%286194226738%29.jpg/440px-ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_%286194226738%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ERA_Aviation_prop_plane_landing_at_ANC_(6194226738).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Bombardier_Dash_8" title="Bombardier Dash 8">Bombardier Dash 8</a>, operated by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Era_Alaska" title="Era Alaska">Era Alaska</a>, on approach to <a href="/wiki/Ted_Stevens_Anchorage_International_Airport" title="Ted Stevens Anchorage International Airport">Ted Stevens Anchorage International Airport</a>.</div></div></div>\n<p>The bulk of remaining commercial flight offerings come from small regional commuter airlines such as <a href="/wiki/Ravn_Alaska" title="Ravn Alaska">Ravn Alaska</a>, <a href="/wiki/PenAir" title="PenAir">PenAir</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Frontier_Flying_Service" title="Frontier Flying Service">Frontier Flying Service</a>. The smallest towns and villages must rely on scheduled or chartered bush flying services using general aviation aircraft such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cessna_Caravan" title="Cessna Caravan">Cessna Caravan</a>, the most popular aircraft in use in the state. Much of this service can be attributed to the Alaska bypass mail program which subsidizes bulk mail delivery to Alaskan rural communities. The program requires 70% of that subsidy to go to carriers who offer passenger service to the communities.\n</p><p>Many communities have small air taxi services. These operations originated from the demand for customized transport to remote areas. Perhaps the most quintessentially Alaskan plane is the bush seaplane. The world\'s busiest seaplane base is <a href="/wiki/Lake_Hood_Seaplane_Base" title="Lake Hood Seaplane Base">Lake Hood</a>, located next to Ted Stevens Anchorage International Airport, where flights bound for remote villages without an airstrip carry passengers, cargo, and many items from stores and warehouse clubs. In 2006 Alaska had the highest number of pilots per capita of any U.S. state.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_transport">Other transport</span></h3>\n<p>Another Alaskan transportation method is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dogsled" title="Dogsled">dogsled</a>. In modern times (that is, any time after the mid-late 1920s), dog <a href="/wiki/Mushing" title="Mushing">mushing</a> is more of a sport than a true means of transportation. Various races are held around the state, but the best known is the <a href="/wiki/Iditarod_Trail_Sled_Dog_Race" title="Iditarod Trail Sled Dog Race">Iditarod Trail Sled Dog Race</a>, a 1,150-mile (1,850&#160;km) trail from Anchorage to Nome (although the distance varies from year to year, the official distance is set at 1,049 miles or 1,688&#160;km). The race commemorates the famous <a href="/wiki/1925_serum_run_to_Nome" title="1925 serum run to Nome">1925 serum run to Nome</a> in which mushers and dogs like <a href="/wiki/Togo_(dog)" title="Togo (dog)">Togo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Balto" title="Balto">Balto</a> took much-needed medicine to the <a href="/wiki/Diphtheria" title="Diphtheria">diphtheria</a>-stricken community of <a href="/wiki/Nome,_Alaska" title="Nome, Alaska">Nome</a> when all other means of transportation had failed. Mushers from all over the world come to Anchorage each March to compete for cash, prizes, and prestige. The "Serum Run" is another sled dog race that more accurately follows the route of the famous 1925 relay, leaving from the community of <a href="/wiki/Nenana,_Alaska" title="Nenana, Alaska">Nenana</a> (southwest of Fairbanks) to Nome.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>In areas not served by road or rail, primary transportation in summer is by <a href="/wiki/All-terrain_vehicle" title="All-terrain vehicle">all-terrain vehicle</a> and in winter by <a href="/wiki/Snowmobile" title="Snowmobile">snowmobile</a> or "snow machine", as it is commonly referred to in Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Data_transport">Data transport</span></h3>\n<p>Alaska\'s internet and other data transport systems are provided largely through the two major telecommunications companies: <a href="/wiki/GCI_(company)" title="GCI (company)">GCI</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Communications" title="Alaska Communications">Alaska Communications</a>. GCI owns and operates what it calls the Alaska United Fiber Optic system<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> and as of late 2011 Alaska Communications advertised that it has "two fiber optic paths to the lower 48 and two more across Alaska.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> In January 2011, it was reported that a $1 billion project to connect Asia and rural Alaska was being planned, aided in part by $350 million in stimulus from the federal government.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Law_and_government">Law and government</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_government">State government</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Juneau,_Alaska_Downtown.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2112" data-file-width="2816" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Juneau%2C_Alaska_Downtown.jpg/220px-Juneau%2C_Alaska_Downtown.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Juneau%2C_Alaska_Downtown.jpg/330px-Juneau%2C_Alaska_Downtown.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5f/Juneau%2C_Alaska_Downtown.jpg/440px-Juneau%2C_Alaska_Downtown.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Juneau,_Alaska_Downtown.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The center of state government in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Juneau" title="Juneau">Juneau</a>. The large buildings in the background are, from left to right: the Court Plaza Building (known colloquially as the "<a href="/wiki/Spam_(food)" title="Spam (food)">Spam Can</a>"), the State Office Building (behind), the Alaska Office Building, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Justices_of_the_Alaska_Supreme_Court" title="List of Justices of the Alaska Supreme Court">John H. Dimond</a> State Courthouse, and the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_State_Capitol" title="Alaska State Capitol">Alaska State Capitol</a>. Many of the smaller buildings in the foreground are also occupied by state government agencies.</div></div></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Government_of_Alaska" title="Government of Alaska">Government of Alaska</a></div>\n<p>Like all other U.S. states, Alaska is governed as a republic, with three <a href="/wiki/Separation_of_powers" title="Separation of powers">branches of government</a>: an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Executive_branch" title="Executive branch">executive branch</a> consisting of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_of_Alaska" title="Governor of Alaska">Governor of Alaska</a> and his appointees which head executive departments; a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Legislative_branch" title="Legislative branch">legislative branch</a> consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_House_of_Representatives" title="Alaska House of Representatives">Alaska House of Representatives</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Senate" title="Alaska Senate">Alaska Senate</a>; and a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judicial_branch" title="Judicial branch">judicial branch</a> consisting of the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Supreme_Court" title="Alaska Supreme Court">Alaska Supreme Court</a> and lower courts.\n</p><p>The state of Alaska employs approximately 16,000 people statewide.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Legislature" title="Alaska Legislature">Alaska Legislature</a> consists of a 40-member <a href="/wiki/Alaska_House_of_Representatives" title="Alaska House of Representatives">House of Representatives</a> and a 20-member <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Senate" title="Alaska Senate">Senate</a>. Senators serve four-year terms and House members two. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Governor_of_Alaska" title="Governor of Alaska">Governor of Alaska</a> serves four-year terms. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Lieutenant_Governors_of_Alaska" title="List of Lieutenant Governors of Alaska">lieutenant governor</a> runs separately from the governor in the <a href="/wiki/Primary_election" title="Primary election">primaries</a>, but during the general election, the nominee for governor and nominee for lieutenant governor run together on the same ticket.\n</p><p>Alaska\'s court system has four levels: the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Supreme_Court" title="Alaska Supreme Court">Alaska Supreme Court</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Court_of_Appeals" title="Alaska Court of Appeals">Alaska Court of Appeals</a>, the superior courts and the district courts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cts_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-cts-106">[106]</a></sup> The superior and district courts are <a href="/wiki/Trial_court" title="Trial court">trial courts</a>. Superior courts are courts of general jurisdiction, while district courts only hear certain types of cases, including misdemeanor criminal cases and civil cases valued up to $100,000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cts_106-1"><a href="#cite_note-cts-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Supreme Court and the Court of Appeals are <a href="/wiki/Appellate_court" title="Appellate court">appellate courts</a>. The Court of Appeals is required to hear appeals from certain lower-court decisions, including those regarding criminal prosecutions, juvenile delinquency, and <a href="/wiki/Habeas_corpus" title="Habeas corpus">habeas corpus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cts_106-2"><a href="#cite_note-cts-106">[106]</a></sup> The Supreme Court hears civil appeals and may in its discretion hear criminal appeals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cts_106-3"><a href="#cite_note-cts-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="State_politics">State politics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Politics_of_Alaska" title="Politics of Alaska">Politics of Alaska</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote gubernatorial election, 2014">2014</a>\n</td>\n<td align="center" style="background:#B0CEFF"><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[a]</a></sup>\n</td>\n<td style="background:#FFB6B6">45.9% <i>128,435</i>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td style="background:#FFB6B6"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_gubernatorial_election,_2018" title="Alaska gubernatorial election, 2018">2018</a>\n</td>\n<td style="background:#B0CEFF">44.4% <i>125,739</i>\n</td>\n<td style="background:#FFB6B6"><b>51.4%</b> <i>145,631</i>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Although in its early years of statehood Alaska was a <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democratic</a> state, since the early 1970s it has been characterized as <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a>-leaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup> Local political communities have often worked on issues related to land use development, fishing, tourism, and individual rights. <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Natives" title="Alaska Natives">Alaska Natives</a>, while organized in and around their communities, have been active within the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alaska_Native_Regional_Corporations" title="Alaska Native Regional Corporations">Native corporations</a>. These have been given ownership over large tracts of land, which require stewardship.\n</p><p>Alaska was formerly the only state in which possession of one ounce or less of marijuana in one\'s home was completely legal under state law, though the federal law remains in force.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-seattle_times_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-seattle_times-110">[109]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The state has an independence movement favoring a vote on secession from the United States, with the <a href="/wiki/Alaskan_Independence_Party" title="Alaskan Independence Party">Alaskan Independence Party</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AIPqa_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-AIPqa-111">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Six <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republicans</a> and four <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Party_(United_States)" title="Democratic Party (United States)">Democrats</a> have served as governor of Alaska. In addition, Republican Governor <a href="/wiki/Wally_Hickel" title="Wally Hickel">Wally Hickel</a> was elected to the office for a second term in 1990 after leaving the Republican party and briefly joining the Alaskan Independence Party ticket just long enough to be reelected. He officially rejoined the Republican party in 1994.\n</p><p>Alaska\'s voter initiative making marijuana legal took effect on February 24, 2015, placing Alaska alongside Colorado and Washington as the first three U.S. states where recreational marijuana is legal. The new law means people over age 21 can consume small amounts of pot &#8211; if they can find it. There is a rather lengthy and involved application process, per  <a href="/wiki/Alaska_Measure_2_(2014)" title="Alaska Measure 2 (2014)">Alaska Measure 2 (2014)</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup> The first legal marijuana store opened in Valdez in October 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxes">Taxes</span></h3>\n<p>To finance state government operations, Alaska depends primarily on petroleum revenues and federal subsidies. This allows it to have the lowest individual tax burden in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> It is one of five states with no state <a href="/wiki/Sales_tax" title="Sales tax">sales tax</a>, one of seven states that do not levy an individual <a href="/wiki/Income_tax" title="Income tax">income tax</a>, and one of the two states that has neither (New Hampshire is the other one)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup> The Department of Revenue Tax Division<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup> reports regularly on the state\'s revenue sources. The Department also issues an annual summary of its operations, including new state laws that directly affect the tax division.\n</p><p>While Alaska has no state sales tax, 89 municipalities collect a local sales tax, from 1.0&#8211;7.5%, typically 3&#8211;5%. Other local taxes levied include raw fish taxes, hotel, motel, and bed-and-breakfast \'bed\' taxes, <a href="/wiki/Severance_tax" title="Severance tax">severance taxes</a>, liquor and tobacco taxes, gaming (pull tabs) taxes, tire taxes and fuel transfer taxes. A part of the revenue collected from certain state taxes and license fees (such as petroleum, aviation motor fuel, telephone cooperative) is shared with municipalities in Alaska.\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Fairbanks,_Alaska" title="Fairbanks, Alaska">Fairbanks</a> has one of the highest property taxes in the state as no sales or income taxes are assessed in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fairbanks_North_Star_Borough" title="Fairbanks North Star Borough">Fairbanks North Star Borough</a> (FNSB). A sales tax for the FNSB has been voted on many times, but has yet to be approved, leading lawmakers to increase taxes dramatically on goods such as liquor and tobacco.\n</p><p>In 2014 the <a href="/wiki/Tax_Foundation" title="Tax Foundation">Tax Foundation</a> ranked Alaska as having the fourth most "business friendly" tax policy, behind only <a href="/wiki/Wyoming" title="Wyoming">Wyoming</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nevada" title="Nevada">Nevada</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Federal_politics">Federal politics</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article:
